Title: Lyapunov-Based Sample Complexity Analysis for Weakly-Coupled MDPs

URL Source: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.14095v2

Published Time: Tue, 16 Jun 2026 01:36:06 GMT

Number of Pages: 71

Markdown Content:
# Lyapunov-Based Sample Complexity Analysis for Weakly-Coupled MDPs 

Tianhao Wu 

Department of Industrial and Systems Engineering, University of Wisconsin-Madison, tianhao.wu@wisc.edu 

Matthew Zurek 

Department of Computer Sciences, University of Wisconsin-Madison, matthew.zurek@wisc.edu 

Weina Wang 

Computer Science Department, Carnegie Mellon University, weinaw@cs.cmu.edu 

Qiaomin Xie 

Department of Industrial and Systems Engineering, University of Wisconsin-Madison, qiaomin.xie@wisc.edu 

We study the sample complexity of learning in average-reward weakly-coupled Markov decision processes (WCMDPs) and Restless Bandits (RBs) under a generative model. Naive reduction to a tabular MDP leads to high complexity bounds as the state-action space is exponentially large in the number of arms 𝑁 . By exploiting the weakly coupled structure, we show that near-optimal policies can be learned with sample and computational complexities that are polynomial in 𝑁 . Specifically, we analyze the plug-in approach, which applies an efficient planning algorithm to an empirical model estimated from data. For fully heterogeneous WCMDPs, we establish the first finite-sample PAC guarantee with polynomial complexity and an 𝑂 (1/√𝑁 ) optimality gap. For homogeneous RBs, we further prove that a smaller optimality gap is achievable under mild structural assumptions. A primary technical contribution of our work is a novel Lyapunov-based analysis framework. Unlike classical approaches that rely on the difficult-to-control bias function, our framework uses an explicitly constructed Lyapunov function along with a drift transfer technique between the true and empirical models. A key step of independent interest in our framework is a fine-grained perturbation analysis for the underlying linear programming (LP) relaxation, which provides a general tool for analyzing LP-based policies and weakly-coupled systems. 

Key words : Weakly-coupled MDPs, restless bandits, sample complexity, Lyapunov analysis. 

1. Introduction 

Large-scale decision-making problems with shared resources arise throughout modern engineering systems, including online advertising (Boutilier and Lu 2016, Zhou et al. 2023), machine mainte-nance (Glazebrook et al. 2005), job scheduling (Yu et al. 2018), surveillance (Villar 2016), and healthcare operations (Biswas et al. 2021). A common modeling paradigm for these complex systems is a weakly-coupled Markov decision process (WCMDP) (Hawkins 2003), which comprises 𝑁 

arms/subsystems, each of which itself is an MDP. In a heterogeneous WCMDP, each arm evolves according to its own dynamics, yet their actions are coupled through several global constraints. The 

Accepted for presentation at the Conference on Learning Theory (COLT) 2026 

1

> arXiv:2606.14095v2 [cs.LG] 15 Jun 2026 2

goal is to find a policy that maximizes the long-term average reward over an infinite time horizon. A prominent special case is the (homogeneous) restless bandit (RB) model (Whittle 1988), where all arms share the same MDP model with a binary action space (active/passive). In RBs, there is only one constraint that limits the total number of active arms at each time step. Learning in WCMDPs and RBs is challenging: when viewed as a single MDP, the state-action space S × A of a WCMDP/RB is the product of the state-action spaces of the arms and thus has cardinality exponentially large in the number of arms 𝑁 . Recent work has essentially resolved the sample complexity of tabular average-reward MDPs and established the necessity of a Θ(| S × A|) 

sample size (Wang et al. 2022, 2024a, Zurek and Chen 2025b,a). Naively applying these results to WCMDPs and RBs, which ignores the weakly coupling structure, yields a sample complexity exponential in 𝑁 . Even the planning problem—finding the exact optimal policy when the transition dynamics are known—is computationally intractable due to large spaces. Nonetheless, recent work has made notable progress on the planning problem and developed near-optimal policies that can be computed efficiently (Zhang et al. 2025, Hong et al. 2024a,b, Yan et al. 2024, Gast et al. 2024). This paper considers the learning setting and addresses the following key question: 

How can one learn a near-optimal policy in average-reward WCMDPs (or RBs) without incurring exponential dependence on the number of arms 𝑁 ?

We answer this question for both fully heterogeneous WCMDPs and homogeneous RBs with access to a generative model, showing that polynomial-in-𝑁 complexities are possible by exploiting the weakly coupled structure. In particular, we develop a plug-in approach, which applies an efficient planning algorithm—specifically the ID policy (Zhang et al. 2025) for WCMDPs and two-set policy (Hong et al. 2024a) for RBs—to the empirical model estimated from data. For fully heterogeneous WCMDPs, we establish the first finite-sample (PAC) optimality-gap guarantee, where both sample and computational complexities scale polynomially in 𝑁 for obtaining a near-optimal policy with an 𝑂 (1/√𝑁 ) optimality gap. For RBs, we further show that an optimality gap of 

𝑂 (1/𝑁 𝛽 ) is achievable for any 𝛽 > 0 under mild structural assumptions. A main technical contribution of the paper is a new Lyapunov-based analysis framework. Classical sample complexity analysis for the plug-in approach uses the standard simulation lemma, which relies on the bias function (a.k.a. relative value function) of the MDP (Zurek and Chen 2024, Li et al. 2020, Agarwal et al. 2020). The bias function is typically implicit and difficult to control, especially under the sophisticated planning algorithms we adopt. Our approach replaces the role of the bias function with an explicitly constructed Lyapunov function . Specifically, our framework first performs 3

a Lyapunov drift analysis of the adopted planning algorithm in the true system, and then employs a drift transfer technique that allows us to analyze the empirical system by explicitly bounding the norm of the Lyapunov function. This framework applies whenever the planning algorithm admits a Lyapunov drift analysis, thus providing a powerful tool for analyzing the plug-in approach in average-reward weakly-coupled systems. We present the details of this framework in Section 5. As a key step in implementing our Lyapunov framework, we develop a fine-grained perturbation analysis for the linear program (LP) relaxation associated with the RB. The analysis is applicable whenever the LP is solved with perturbed inputs, including but not limited to sampling-based estimates. Beyond serving as a technical tool in our proofs, this perturbation result reveals an intrinsic robustness property of policies constructed using this LP: under appropriate technical conditions, such a policy is stable against perturbed input, in the sense that the actions remain unchanged in all but one state, in which the action probabilities are adjusted to satisfy the constraints under perturbation. Since many control schemes for WCMDPs and RBs rely on solving variants of this LP relaxation, our perturbation results can be used as a robustness module in analyzing other algorithms, including various index and LP-priority policies that are extensively studied in the WCMDP and RB literature (Weber and Weiss 1990, Verloop 2016, Gast et al. 2024, Hodge and Glazebrook 2015).  

> Related work.

We review related work along the following three dimensions. 

Planning for average-reward RBs/WCMDPs. For RBs, a classical starting point is the Whittle index policy (Whittle 1988, Weber and Weiss 1990), which is derived from a Lagrangian relaxation and yields an index rule when the problem is indexable. However, indexability may fail or be difficult to verify in general, and even when it holds, the Whittle index policy is not guaranteed to be optimal for finite systems. Recent work has made progress on asymptotic optimality under average reward: Hong et al. (2024b) show that unichain/aperiodicity conditions are sufficient for asymptotic optimality, while Hong et al. (2024a) obtains an exponential asymptotic optimality gap without global attractor assumptions. Moving beyond RBs to general WCMDPs, Meuleau et al. (1998) propose Markov task decomposition with greedy two-phase heuristics, and recent Lyapunov-based analyses obtain optimality gaps such as 𝑂 (1/√𝑁 ) for fully heterogeneous WCMDPs (Zhang et al. 2025). These results are in the planning regime and do not directly provide finite-sample learning guarantees when the arm dynamics are unknown. 

Learning for average-reward RBs/WCMDPs. One line of work studies online learning for average-reward RBs, focusing on regret guarantees. Early papers considered weak regret against a simple benchmark policy (Dai et al. 2011, Liu et al. 2011, Tekin and Liu 2012). Other results 4

obtain sublinear regret against stronger benchmarks but either incur exponential computational complexity (Ortner et al. 2012) or apply to special RB classes such as birth-death arms (Wang et al. 2020). In multi-action average-reward RBs, Xiong et al. (2022) develop an LP-based index policy that is asymptotically optimal and design learning algorithms with e𝑂 (√𝑇 ) regret against that index policy. Thompson-sampling based algorithms have also been studied (Jung et al. 2019). These regret results do not imply the PAC/sample-complexity guarantees studied here, since there is no general regret-to-PAC conversion for average-reward MDPs (Tuynman et al. 2024). Several papers study convergence of learning algorithms for RBs (Avrachenkov and Borkar 2022, Avrachenkov et al. 2024, Killian et al. 2021) and WCMDPs (El Shar and Jiang 2023), but they do not provide finite-sample optimality-gap bounds. Recent Q-learning methods with function approximation aim to learn Whittle index policies (Xiong and Li 2023, Xiong et al. 2024); their finite-time convergence results do not translate directly to sample-complexity bounds for the optimality gap considered here. For finite-horizon or discounted objectives, methods based on Lagrangian relaxation, Q-learning, and deep RL have also been proposed (Killian et al. 2021, Robledo et al. 2022, Killian et al. 2022, Nakhleh et al. 2021, Robledo et al. 2024), but the guarantees are largely asymptotic or tailored to different performance criteria. 

Sample complexity of tabular average-reward MDPs. Recent work has obtained sharp sample-complexity bounds for single-arm tabular average-reward MDPs. For uniformly mixing or uniformly ergodic MDPs with mixing-time bound 𝑡 mix , Wang et al. (2024b) obtain a minimax-optimal sample complexity e𝑂 (𝑆 𝐴𝑡 mix /𝜀 2), matching lower bounds up to logarithmic factors; see also Jin and Sidford (2021). For weakly communicating MDPs, Zurek and Chen (2025b) establish a minimax-optimal bound e𝑂 (𝑆 𝐴𝐻 /𝜀 2), where 𝐻 = sp (ℎ★) is the span of the optimal bias function. Earlier work by Wang et al. (2022) gave related upper and lower bounds, and the most general multichain setting has been characterized using a transient-time parameter. Parameter-free and adaptive algorithms have also been developed (Tuynman et al. 2024, Neu and Okolo 2024, Zurek and Chen 2024, Lee et al. 2025, Zurek and Chen 2025a). These PAC/sample-complexity guarantees are for single-arm tabular MDPs; applying them naively to an 𝑁 -armed WCMDP or RB leads to exponential dependence on 

𝑁 because the joint state-action space is exponentially large. 

2. Problem Setup  

> 2.1. Weakly-coupled MDPs

A weakly-coupled MDP consists of 𝑁 arms. Each arm 𝑖 ∈ [ 𝑁 ] ≜ {1, 2, . . . , 𝑁 } itself is associated with an MDP M𝑖 = (S , A, 𝑃 𝑖 , 𝑟 𝑖 , (𝑐 𝑘,𝑖 )𝑘 ∈[ 𝐾 ] ). Here S and A are the state and action spaces, respectively, 5

which are finite sets with |S| = 𝑆, |A| = 𝐴 ; 𝑃 𝑖 (𝑠 ′

of each type 𝑘 . We assume that the reward functions take values in [0, 𝑟 max ] for 𝑟 max > 0, and the cost functions take values in [0, 𝑐 max ] for 𝑐 max > 0.For the overall 𝑁 -armed MDP, its state space is S𝑁 and action space is A𝑁 , where the state is the joint state of all arms, denoted as 𝒔 = (𝑠 1, . . . , 𝑠 𝑁 ), the action is the joint action of all arms, denoted as 𝒂 = (𝑎 1, . . . , 𝑎 𝑁 ), and the reward is the sum of rewards from all arms. Given actions, the arms make state transitions independently, i.e., 𝑷 (𝒔 ′ | 𝒔 , 𝒂 ) = Î𝑖 ∈[ 𝑁 ] 𝑃 𝑖 (𝑠 ′

We assume that for every arm, the action 0, paired with any state, does not incur any cost of any type, and thus there always exists a feasible action 𝒂 = (0, 0, . . . , 0).In this paper, we vary the number of arms 𝑁 but keep the following constant: the number of cost types 𝐾 , the budgets 𝛼 𝑘 ’s, the reward upper bound 𝑟 max , and the cost upper bound 𝑐 max .

Performance criterion. We consider the infinite-horizon average-reward criterion. For the 𝑁 -armed system, let 𝑺 𝑡 = (𝑆 𝑖,𝑡 )𝑖 ∈[ 𝑁 ] denote the state at time 𝑡 , where 𝑆 𝑖,𝑡 is the state of arm 𝑖 ; let 𝑨 𝑡 = ( 𝐴 𝑖,𝑡 )𝑖 ∈[ 𝑁 ]

2.3. Restless bandits 

An extensively studied subclass of WCMDPs is the restless bandits. The additional structure in RBs has enabled the development of planning policies with smaller optimality gap. We are interested in whether the similar refinement is possible for learning. We use the same notation for RBs as that for WCMDPs unless otherwise specified. It should be clear what we refer to from the context. An RB problem is a WCMDP where each arm has a binary action space A = {0, 1}, where 𝑎 = 1 is referred to as the pulling/active action and 𝑎 = 0 the 

non-pulling/passive action. The cost constraint in an RB has a simple form: ∑︁  

> 𝑖 ∈[ 𝑁 ]

𝑎 𝑖 = 𝛼𝑁, 

where 0 < 𝛼 < 1 and 𝛼𝑁 is an integer. Compared to the standard WCMDP form, this constraint is written as an equality rather than an inequality. This discrepancy is largely conventional and does not change the problem in any fundamental way. We focus on the commonly studied homogeneous 

setting, where each arm’s MDP has the same transition kernel 𝑃 and reward function 𝑟 .An RB has an LP relaxation similar to that of the WCMDP (Hong et al. 2024a):

3. Learning via a Plug-in Approach with Reference Policies 

We adopt a plug-in approach for learning. This approach first constructs an empirical transition probability matrix for each arm using data, and then plug the empirical MDP into a planning algorithm. While the exact optimal policy is intractable for WCMDPs/RBs, we make use of existing efficient planning algorithms for computing a near-optimal policy, which we call a reference policy .

3.1. Constructing empirical MDPs 

We assume access to a generative model, also known as a simulator (Kearns and Singh 1998), for the transition matrix of each arm’s MDP. As is standard in the generative setting, we assume the reward and cost functions are known. In an 𝑁 -armed WCMDP, for each arm 𝑖 , we collect 𝑛 i.i.d. samples 𝑆 𝑖, 1

> 𝑠,𝑎

, . . . , 𝑆 𝑖,𝑛 𝑠,𝑎 from 𝑃 𝑖 (· | 𝑠, 𝑎 )

for each state-action pair (𝑠, 𝑎 ) ∈ S × A . We construct the empirical transition kernel b𝑃 𝑖 by 

b𝑃 𝑖 (𝑠 ′ | 𝑠, 𝑎 ) ≜ 1

𝑛 i.i.d. samples per state-action pair. Then we define the empirical 𝑁 -armed transition kernel b𝑷 in the same way as WCMDP by setting b𝑃 𝑖 = b𝑃 for all 𝑖 ∈ [ 𝑁 ].

3.2. Plug-in approach for WCMDPs with ID policy 

For WCMDPs, we consider the ID policy developed in Zhang et al. (2025) as our reference policy, denoted as 𝜋 ID . The ID policy is efficiently computable (with poly (𝑁 ) complexity), and (under proper conditions) it achieves an 𝑂 (1/√𝑁 ) optimality gap, i.e., 𝜌 ★(𝒔 0) − 𝜌 𝜋 ID (𝒔 0) = 𝑂 (1/√𝑁 ).A key component of the ID policy is the collection of optimal single-armed policies . Specifically, recall that (𝑦 ★𝑖 (𝑠, 𝑎 )) 𝑖 ∈[ 𝑁 ],𝑠 ∈S ,𝑎 ∈A is an optimal solution to the LP (2). Then for each arm 𝑖 , viewed as an MDP on its own, we can define the following policy: 8

(4) This policy is called the optimal single-armed policy for arm 𝑖 . The policies { ¯𝜋 ★𝑖 }𝑖 ∈[ 𝑁 ] are designed such that if each arm 𝑖 follows ¯𝜋 ★𝑖 and evolves completely independently, then the arms together satisfy the cost constraint in a time-average fashion and achieve a total reward of 𝜌 rel , the optimal value of the LP, which is an upper bound on the true optimal value of the 𝑁 -armed WCMDP. We defer a formal description of the ID policy to Algorithm 1 in Appendix C. The high-level idea of the ID policy is to let most arms follow their optimal single-armed policies, while conforming to the hard cost constraints. An insight behind this approach is that if the arms are given a consistent ordering, e.g., based on the arm IDs, then the set of arms that can consistently follow their optimal single-armed policies expands over time and ultimately covers all but 𝑂 (√𝑁 ) arms on average. With the ID policy as our reference policy, the plug-in approach proceeds as follows. We substitute the empirical transition kernels b𝑃 𝑖 ’s into the LP (2) . Solving this “empirical LP” gives an optimal solution (b𝑦 ★𝑖 (𝑠, 𝑎 )) 𝑖 ∈[ 𝑁 ],𝑠 ∈S ,𝑎 ∈A and the resulting optimal single-armed policies {b¯𝜋 ★𝑖 }𝑖 ∈[ 𝑁 ] . We then use (b𝑦 ★𝑖 (𝑠, 𝑎 )) 𝑖 ∈[ 𝑁 ],𝑠 ∈S ,𝑎 ∈A and {b¯𝜋 ★𝑖 }𝑖 ∈[ 𝑁 ] to construct the ID policy, denoted as b𝜋 ID .

3.3. Plug-in approach for RBs with two-set policy 

For homogeneous RBs, we consider the two-set policy from Hong et al. (2024a) as our reference policy, denoted as 𝜋 TS . The two-set policy is also efficiently computable, and (under proper conditions) it achieves an optimality gap 𝜌 ★(𝒔 0) − 𝜌 𝜋 TS (𝒔 0) = 𝑂 (exp (− 𝑐𝑁 )) for a constant 𝑐 > 0.Note that such an exponentially small optimality gap has been known to be achievable only for the RB subclass of WCMDPs, in the homogeneous setting under a set of conditions. The two-set policy is also built on an optimal single-armed policy. Recall that (𝑦 ★(𝑠, 𝑎 )) 𝑠 ∈S ,𝑎 ∈A 

is an optimal solution to the LP (3). We define the corresponding optimal single-armed policy as:

We first substitute the empirical kernel b𝑃 into the LP in (3) . Solving this LP gives an optimal solution (b𝑦 ★(𝑠, 𝑎 )) 𝑠 ∈S ,𝑎 ∈A and the corresponding optimal single-armed policies b¯𝜋 ★. We then use (b𝑦 ★(𝑠, 𝑎 )) 𝑠 ∈S ,𝑎 ∈A and b¯𝜋 ★ to construct the two-set policy, which is denoted as b𝜋 TS .

4. Main Results 

We present our main results, which give upper bounds on the optimality gaps of the learned policies: 

b𝜋 ID for WCMDPs and b𝜋 TS for RBs. These bounds directly imply sample complexity bounds.  

> 4.1. Main Results for WCMDPs

We begin by stating our assumptions. In average-reward MDP literature, it is common for sample complexity results to require a uniform mixing time bound under all policies (e.g., Jin and Sidford 2020, 2021, Wang et al. 2023, Li et al. 2024), or alternatively depend on the MDP diameter or bias span of an optimal policy (e.g., Zurek and Chen 2025b, 2024, Tuynman et al. 2024). In our setting, the weakly coupled structure allows us to avoid imposing uniform mixing (or other) assumptions on the overall 𝑁 -armed MDP, whose state/action spaces grow exponentially with 𝑁 . Instead, it suffices to make uniform mixing assumption on the individual arm MDPs. Specifically, for each arm 𝑖 , we consider the associated single-armed MDP (S , A, 𝑃 𝑖 , 𝑟 𝑖 ), ignoring the cost functions and constraints. Given a policy 𝜋 for this MDP, let 𝑃 𝜋 𝑖 denote the transition probability matrix of the Markov chain induced by 𝜋 , i.e., 𝑃 𝜋 𝑖 (𝑠, 𝑠 ′) := Í𝑎 ∈A 𝑃 𝑖 (𝑠 ′ | 𝑠, 𝑎 )𝜋 (𝑎 | 𝑠 ).

If this Markov chain has a unique stationary distribution 𝜇 𝜋 𝑖 , we define its mixing time as 𝜏 𝜋 𝑖 :=

min 𝑡 ∈ N : max 𝑠 ∈S (𝑃 𝜋 𝑖 )𝑡 (𝑠, ·) − 𝜇 𝜋 𝑖 (·) 1 ≤ 1/4 , where (𝑃 𝜋 𝑖 )𝑡 is the 𝑡 -step transition matrix. We assume that each arm has a finite uniform mixing time bound under all policies, and further that these bounds across arms have a finite uniform upper bound, as summarized in Assumption 1 below. 

Assumption 1 (Unichain and Uniform Mixing) . We assume that for each arm 𝑖 , its associated single-armed MDP (S , A, 𝑃 𝑖 , 𝑟 𝑖 ) is unichain; i.e., all policies induce a Markov chain with a single recurrent class (and possibly some transient states). Further, we assume that there exists a finite 

𝜏 unif such that sup 𝑁 ∈N+,𝑖 ∈[ 𝑁 ] sup 𝜋 𝜏 𝜋 𝑖 ≤ 𝜏 unif .

We remark that our learning approach does not use prior knowledge of the mixing time upper bound 𝜏 unif in Assumption 1. 10 

Theorem 1. Consider an 𝑁 -armed WCMDP that satisfies Assumption 1. Let b𝜋 ID be the learned policy using the plug-in approach with the ID policy as the reference policy, and let 𝑛 be the number of samples per state-action pair per arm. Then for any initial state 𝒔 0, with probability at least 1 − 𝜂 ,

𝜌 ★(𝒔 0) − 𝜌 b𝜋 ID (𝒔 0) ≤ min

𝐶 1√︁ 𝑆 + log (𝑆 𝐴𝑁 /𝜂 ) 𝑁 

√𝑛 , 𝑟 max

+ 𝐶 2

√𝑁 ,

where 𝐶 1 and 𝐶 2 are constants independent of 𝑁 , 𝑛 , 𝜂 , and 𝒔 0.

We provide a proof of Theorem 1 in Section 6, and give the detailed proofs of the key lemmas in Appendix D. The optimality gap bound for b𝜋 ID in Theorem 1 consists of two terms. The first term is a finite-sample error term that decreases at rate 1/√𝑛 as the sample size 𝑛 increases. The second term, 𝐶 2/√𝑁 , is inherited from the optimality gap of the ID policy itself and is not affected by learning or the sample size 𝑛 . However, it vanishes at rate 1/√𝑁 as the system size 𝑁 increases. This scaling is the asymptotic regime of interest for WCMDPs, where the primary interest is in large systems. The bound in Theorem 1 directly yields a sample complexity bound for achieving an optimality gap at most 𝜖 . Note that we can only guarantee a gap level 𝜖 = Ω (1/√𝑁 ) due to the second term in the bound. To achieve any 𝜖 = Ω (1/√𝑁 ), the sample size needed is 𝑛 = Ω 

 (𝑆 +log (𝑆 𝐴𝑁 /𝜂 )) 𝑁 2

> 𝜖 2



.Notably, the total number of samples needed, 𝑁𝑆 𝐴𝑛 , grows only polynomially in 𝑁 rather than exponentially in 𝑁 , although the state and action space sizes of the overall 𝑁 -armed MDP are exponential in 𝑁 . This contrasts with the sample complexity results for general average-reward MDPs, and formally demonstrates the benefit of exploiting the weakly coupled structure in learning.  

> 4.2. Main Results for RBs

Recall that for RBs, our plug-in learning approach uses the two-set policy as the reference policy. We first state a set of assumptions slightly adapted from the conditions that guarantee the 𝑂 (exp (− 𝑐𝑁 )) 

optimality gap for the two-set policy (Hong et al. 2024a). Recall that (𝑦 ★(𝑠, 𝑎 )) 𝑠 ∈S ,𝑎 ∈A is an optimal solution to the LP (3) , based on which we defined the optimal single-armed policy ¯𝜋 ★ in (5) . Let 𝜇 ★(𝑠 ) := 𝑦 ★(𝑠, 0) + 𝑦 ★(𝑠, 1) for all 𝑠 ∈ S . It is easy to verify that 𝜇 ★ is a stationary distribution for the single-armed MDP under policy ¯𝜋 ★. With these quantities, we define an 𝑆 × 𝑆 matrix Φ as follows

Assumption 2. For an RB with single-armed MDP (S , A, 𝑃, 𝑟 ) and cost budget 𝛼 , we assume: 

• (Ergodicity) For the single-armed MDP, the Markov chain induced by the optimal single-armed policy ¯𝜋 ★ is aperiodic and irreducible and has a mixing time upper bounded by 𝜏 .

• (Non-degeneracy) There exists a unique state ˜𝑠 ∈ S such that 𝑦 ★( ˜𝑠, 1) > 0 and 𝑦 ★( ˜𝑠, 0) > 0. This state is referred to as the neutral state. 

• (Local stability) The matrix Φ satisfies ∥Φ∥𝜇 ★→𝜇 ★ < 1.

We remark that these assumptions are slightly stronger than those in Hong et al. (2024a). In particular, the ergodicity assumption requires irreducibility while that paper requires unichain; the local stability assumption here is also slightly stronger since Hong et al. (2024a) assumes the moduli of all eigenvalues of Φ are strictly less than 1.We make a further assumption on the dual LP of the LP (3) , where the dual LP can be written as

Assumption 3 (Strict action-value gaps) . There exists an optimal solution (𝜁 ★, 𝜆 ★, ℎ ★) to the dual LP (7) which satisfies that for all 𝑠 ∈ S , 𝑎 ∈ A such that 𝑦 ★(𝑠, 𝑎 ) = 0, letting ¯𝑎 = 1 − 𝑎 , we have

Assumption 3 is a dual non-degeneracy condition, which in general is a sufficient condition for a unique primal solution, and in our setting is actually an equivalent condition. We highlight that we do not require uniform-mixing type assumptions. Instead, we only require ergodicity for the single-armed Markov chain induced by one policy , ¯𝜋 ★. Therefore, our analysis of the learned policy differs from established techniques based on uniform mixing. Interestingly, the assumptions that allow for an exponentially small optimality gap, after slightly strengthened into our Assumptions 2 and 3, also enables us to prove a perturbation analysis theorem (Theorem 3). This theorem shows that the single-armed policy b¯𝜋 ★ solved from the empirical LP preserves key structural properties, including the identity of the neutral state and the local stability property under small transition-kernel perturbations. This establishes a form of robustness induced by the structural assumptions. We view this perturbation result as potentially of independent interest. The 12 

full statement of Theorem 3 is given in Section 4.3, the proof is given in Section 8. Based on Theorem 3, we obtain the main theorem for RBs. The proof is provided in Section 7, and the detailed proofs of the key lemmas are in Appendix G.

Similarly to WCMDP, the optimality gap bound for b𝜋 TS in Theorem 2 also consists of two terms. The first term is the finite-sample error that decreases at a rate 1/√𝑛 with the sample size 𝑛 . The second term, 𝐶 4 exp (− 𝐶 5 𝑁 ), is inherited from the optimality gap of the two-set policy. The bound in Theorem 2 also directly yields a sample complexity bound for achieving an optimality gap at most 𝜖 . > Blocking in the fixed-sample regime.

The bound in Theorem 2 also suggests a simple modification when the sample size 𝑛 is fixed, as in an offline setting. We partition the 𝑁 arms into 𝐵 disjoint blocks, each of size ¯𝑁 = 𝑁 /𝐵 , and run the plug-in two-set policy independently within each block. Since the RB is homogeneous, every block uses the same normalized budget 𝛼 , so the block budgets add up to the original budget. The blocked policy, denoted by b𝜋 blk TS , simply combines the actions chosen by the block-level policies. We only use block sizes for which Theorem 2 can still be applied. Let 𝑁 max be the fixed lower bound on the system size in that theorem. Choose a sequence 𝑁 blk = 𝜔 (1) with 𝑁 blk ≥ 𝑁 max , and restrict to ¯𝑁 = 𝑁 𝐵 ≥ 𝑁 blk . The role of 𝑁 blk is just to rule out blocks that are too small. If the blocks are too small, the large-𝑁 guarantee for the two-set policy no longer applies. Equivalently, we require

5. For all 𝑠, 𝑎 such that b𝑦 ★(𝑠, 𝑎 ) = 0, we have 

b𝜁 ★(𝑠 ) + bℎ★(𝑠 ) − 𝑟 (𝑠, 𝑎 ) − b𝜆 ★I(𝑎 = 1) − b𝑃 𝑠𝑎 bℎ★ ≥ 𝜁 ★(𝑠 ) + ℎ★(𝑠 ) − 𝑟 (𝑠, 𝑎 ) − 𝜆 ★I(𝑎 = 1) − 𝑃 𝑠𝑎 ℎ★

2 .

6. The Markov chain induced by b¯𝜋 ★ under b𝑃 is aperiodic and irreducible, with stationary distribution b𝜇 ★ and mixing time at most (3 + log 2 𝑆 )𝜏 . 

> Interpretation of Theorem 3.

Theorem 3 describes how the LP solution changes when the transition kernel 𝑃 is replaced by b𝑃 . Under Assumptions 2 and 3, the empirical LP preserves the main structural properties of the true LP. Parts 1 and 2 show that the support structure of the optimal LP solution is stable. In particular, 

b𝑦 ★(𝑠, 𝑎 ) = 0 ⇐⇒ 𝑦 ★(𝑠, 𝑎 ) = 0. Therefore, by the construction in (5) , the empirical optimal single-arm policy can differ from the true optimal single-arm policy only at the neutral state. The neutral state is also preserved: ˜𝑠 remains the unique state where both actions have positive mass. This matters because the two-set policy uses the neutral state to adjust the number of active arms and meet the budget constraint. Part 2 further shows that the positive entries of 𝑦 ★ stay bounded away from zero after perturbation. Thus the empirical LP keeps a nontrivial amount of mass on the actions used by the true LP. Parts 3–5 provide the stability bounds used in the Lyapunov analysis. Part 3 shows that local stability is preserved: 1 − ∥ bΦ∥𝜇 ★→𝜇 ★ ≥ 1−∥ Φ∥𝜇 ★→𝜇 ★ 

> 2

.This lets us define the empirical versions of the weighted matrices and the Lyapunov function. Part 4 gives explicit 𝑂 (𝛿 ) bounds on the changes in the primal and dual LP solutions, including b𝑦 ★, bℎ★, b𝜆 ★, and b𝜁 ★. These bounds are then used in Part 5 to show that inactive actions still have positive dual slack after perturbation. Finally, Part 6 shows that the Markov chain induced by the empirical single-arm policy b¯𝜋 ★ under the empirical kernel b𝑃 is ergodic, with a controlled mixing time. This makes sure that the empirical stationary distribution and the empirical Lyapunov function are well-defined. 16 

5. A Lyapunov Framework for Sample Complexity Analysis 

In this section, we give an overview of our technical framework, which we believe is broadly applicable beyond WCMDPs and RBs. Our approach can be viewed as generalizing the classical simulation lemma. A key innovation is that we use Lyapunov drift bounds , rather than the Bellman equation, to derive performance difference between the true and empirical systems. Let 𝜋 be a reference policy and b𝜋 be its empirical version computed from the empirical transition kernels. We use b· to denote the empirical version of a quantity in general, and we ignore the initial state for simplicity. Our goal is to bound the optimality gap of b𝜋 , which can be decomposed as 

𝜌 ★ − 𝜌 b𝜋 ≤ 𝜌 rel − b𝜌 𝜋 + b𝜌 𝜋 − 𝜌 b𝜋 ≤  𝜌 rel − b𝜌 𝜋  +  b𝜌 rel − 𝜌 b𝜋 . (15) In the remainder of this section, we demonstrate our framework on deriving an upper bound for 

𝜌 rel − b𝜌 𝜋 ; the term b𝜌 rel − 𝜌 b𝜋 can be bounded in a similar manner. If we were to use the classical simulation lemma, we would note 𝜌 rel − b𝜌 𝜋 =  𝜌 rel −

𝜌 𝜋  +  𝜌 𝜋 − b𝜌 𝜋 , and then bound the second term as 𝜌 𝜋 − b𝜌 𝜋 = bE𝜋 (𝑷 𝜋 − b𝑷 𝜋 )ℎ𝜋 (𝑍 ∞) ≤

𝑷 𝜋 − b𝑷 𝜋  

> ∞→∞

E [∥ ℎ𝜋 (𝑍 ∞)∥ ∞], where ℎ𝜋 is the bias function of 𝜋 , and 𝑍 ∞ is the system state in steady state. However, a challenge to further proceed is that it is difficult to bound ∥ℎ𝜋 (·)∥ ∞, as the bias function ℎ𝜋 can be complicated. In our approach, we construct a Lyapunov function 𝑉 explicitly, which essentially plays the role of the bias function ℎ𝜋 . However, because 𝑉 is a design choice, we can choose it to ensure ∥𝑉 (·)∥ ∞

has a desirable bound. In particular, our framework has two steps, described below.  

> Step 1: Lyapunov analysis

This step follows the classical Lyapunov analysis, which has a long history in the study of stochastic systems. The goal is to construct a Lyapunov function 𝑉 that satisfies the following two conditions. (C1 ) Drift bound: For each state 𝑧 ,

[( 𝑷 𝜋 − 𝐼 )𝑉 ] ( 𝑧 ) ≤ − 𝐶 0𝐹 (𝑧 ) + 𝐺 1 (𝑁 ), (16) where 𝐹 is a function of the state, 𝐶 0 > 0 is a constant independent of 𝑁 , and 𝐺 1 (𝑁 ) is a term that may depend on 𝑁 .(C2 ) Gap dominance: For each state 𝑧 ,

𝜌 rel − 𝑟 𝜋 (𝑧 ) ≤ 𝐶 ′

> 0

𝐹 (𝑧 ) + 𝐺 2 (𝑁 ), (17) where 𝑟 𝜋 (𝑧 ) = E𝐴 ∼𝜋 (·| 𝑧 ) [𝑟 (𝑧, 𝐴 )] , 𝐶 ′ 

> 0

> 0 is a constant independent of 𝑁 , and 𝐺 2 (𝑁 ) is a term that may depend on 𝑁 .17 

With these conditions, one can bound the optimality gap of the policy 𝜋 . Specifically, we take expectations on both sides of (16) and (17) with respect to 𝑧 ∼ 𝑍 ∞, where 𝑍 ∞ follows the state stationary distribution of 𝜋 . This makes the left-hand-sides of (16) and (17) equal to 0 and 

𝜌 rel − 𝜌 𝜋 , respectively. Combining the two inequalities then yields 𝜌 rel − 𝜌 𝜋 ≤ 𝐶 ′

This approach has been used in Zhang et al. (2025) and Hong et al. (2024a) to derive optimality gap bounds for the ID policy and the two-set policy, respectively.  

> Step 2: Drift transfer

We next transfer the drift bound in (C1) from the true system to the empirical system. The key observation is that, for the same Lyapunov function 𝑉 ,

[( bP𝜋 − 𝐼 )𝑉 ] ( 𝑧 ) = [( P𝜋 − 𝐼 )𝑉 ] ( 𝑧 ) + [( bP𝜋 − P𝜋 )𝑉 ] ( 𝑧 ).

Thus the empirical system inherits the true-system drift bound up to the model-error term [( bP𝜋 −

P𝜋 )𝑉 ] ( 𝑧 ). Therefore, we have: ( ˆC1 ) Drift bound: For each state 𝑧 ,

[( b𝑷 𝜋 − 𝐼 )𝑉 ] ( 𝑧 ) ≤ − 𝐶 0𝐹 (𝑧 ) + 𝐺 1 (𝑁 ) + [( b𝑷 𝜋 − 𝑷 𝜋 )𝑉 ] ( 𝑧 ). (18)  

> Completing the empirical performance bound

With Steps 1 and 2 in place, we are ready to bound the gap, 𝜌 rel − b𝜌 𝜋 , using ( ˆC1 ) and ( C2 ). This follows the same procedure as in deriving an optimality gap bound using ( C1 ) and ( C2 ). We take expectations on both sides of (18) and (17) with respect to 

𝑧 ∼ 𝑍 ∞, where 𝑍 ∞ follows the state stationary distribution of 𝜋 in the empirical system. Again the left-hand-side of (18) becomes 0. This gives

Lemma formalizes this drift-transfer step in a form that we will repeatedly use in the proofs, whose proof is given in Appendix. These steps suggest that our framework may be applicable to a much broader set of problems, given that the reference policy admits a Lyapunov analysis with a well-behaved Lyapunov function. 

6. Proof of Theorem 1 

We employ the framework in Section 5 to prove Theorem 1. According to the decomposition (15) , it suffices to bound 𝜌 rel − b𝜌 𝜋 ID (𝒔 0) and b𝜌 rel − 𝜌 b𝜋 ID (𝒔 0) separately. 18 

Proof roadmap. The proof has three main parts. First, we bound 𝜌 rel − b𝜌 𝜋 ID (𝒔 0), where the ID policy is computed from the true model and evaluated under the empirical model. This step uses the ID policy Lyapunov drift condition from Lemma 5 and the gap dominance bound from Lemma 6, which correspond to Conditions ( C1 ) and ( C2 ) in our framework. Together with the drift transfer technique, they give Lemma 3. Second, we bound b𝜌 rel − 𝜌 b𝜋 ID (𝒔 0), where the ID policy is computed from the empirical model and evaluated under the true model. The argument is parallel, but it first verifies that the empirical single-arm chains are well behaved. The empirical drift and dominance bounds, Lemmas 25 and 26, then give Lemma 4. Both Lemma 3 and Lemma 4 use model-accuracy lemmas, which we state next. Figure 1 summarizes the main proof steps. We omit the auxiliary model-accuracy lemmas from the figure.

> Lemma 6 Gap dominance
> Lemma 3
> Lemma 25 Drift for b𝜋 ID
> Lemma 26 Gap dominance
> Lemma 4
> Theorem 1
> Figure 1 Flowchart for the proof of Theorem 1. We omit auxiliary model-accuracy lemmas.

7. Proof of Theorem 2 

We use the framework in Section 5 to prove Theorem 2. The proof follows the same decomposition as in (15). It is sufficient to bound 𝜌 rel − b𝜌 𝜋 TS (𝒔 0) and b𝜌 rel − 𝜌 b𝜋 TS (𝒔 0).

Proof roadmap. The proof has two main intermediate bounds. First, we bound 𝜌 rel − b𝜌 𝜋 TS (𝒔 0).Here the two-set policy is computed from the true model but is evaluated under the empirical model. We use the Lyapunov drift bound for the two-set policy (Lemma 31) and the gap-dominance bound (Lemma 32). These two lemmas correspond to Conditions ( C1 ) and ( C2 ) in our Lyapunov framework. Together with the drift-transfer argument, they imply Lemma 9. Second, we bound b𝜌 rel − 𝜌 b𝜋 TS (𝒔 0). Here the two-set policy is computed from the empirical model and evaluated under the true model. This part uses the same Lyapunov argument, but but it also uses Theorem 3 to justify the empirical Lyapunov analysis. Then the empirical drift condition (Lemma 39) and the empirical gap dominance bound (Lemma 40) imply Lemma 10. Both intermediate lemmas use model-accuracy lemma, which we state below. Figure 2 summarizes the main steps.

> Lemma 10 Theorem 3

8. Proof of Theorem 3 

In this section we provide the proof of Theorem 3, which will be preceded by many intermediate lemmas, organized into three subsections 8.2, 8.3, 8.4 and 8.5. We firstly give a proof outline in 8.1 to show the high-level ideas.  

> 8.1. Proof outline

The proof uses a primal-dual perturbation argument. Under the nondegeneracy and strict-gap assumptions, a small perturbation of the transition kernel should not change which LP constraints are active. We therefore build candidate primal and dual solutions for the perturbed LP using the same active structure as in the true LP. We then check primal feasibility, dual feasibility, complementary slackness, and uniqueness. One complication is that the RB LP has a redundant flow-balance constraint, and the dual bias vector is only unique up to an additive constant. For this reason, we first perturb the state occupancy instead of perturbing the primal and dual variables directly. Around the optimal state occupancy 𝜇 ★,26 

the locally linear policy follows ¯𝜋 ★ at all non-neutral states and uses the neutral state ˜𝑠 to satisfy the budget. This gives the local linearization matrix 𝑈 = 𝑃 ¯𝜋 ★

− ( 𝑐 ¯𝜋 ★

− 𝛼 1)𝜉 , where 𝜉 = 𝑃 ˜𝑠 1 − 𝑃 ˜𝑠 0.Replacing 𝑃 by b𝑃 gives the perturbed matrix b𝑈 .We define 𝐻 𝑈 = (𝐼 − Φ)−1 − 1𝜇 ★ and b𝜇 = 𝜇 ★  𝐼 − ( b𝑈 − 𝑈 )𝐻 𝑈 

−1. The matrix 𝐻 𝑈 measures how sensitive the state occupancy is to the model perturbation. The key identity is b𝜇 − 𝜇 ★ = b𝜇 ( b𝑈 − 𝑈 )𝐻 𝑈 .

This is similar to the usual perturbation formula for stationary distributions of Markov chains, although 𝑈 and b𝑈 are not transition matrices. After constructing b𝜇 , we use a linear map 𝑊 to define the primal candidate b𝑦 = b𝜇𝑊 . The construction of 𝑊 ensures that b𝑦 satisfies the perturbed budget, flow-balance, and normalization constraints. It also preserves the support pattern: active entries of 𝑦 ★ remain positive, while inactive entries remain zero. For the dual side, we construct (b𝜁, b𝜆, bℎ) so that the perturbed dual constraints are tight on the active state-action pairs. The dual perturbation bounds then show that inactive constraints still have positive slack. Thus the constructed primal and dual candidates are feasible and satisfy complementary slackness, so they are optimal. The strict slack on inactive actions also gives uniqueness and support preservation. Finally, support preservation implies that the empirical optimal single-arm policy can differ from the true one only at the neutral state. Together with a Markov-chain perturbation bound, this gives the aperiodicity, irreducibility, and mixing-time conclusion in the theorem.

We choose b𝜆 so that the same indifference holds for the perturbed dual candidate: 𝑟 ( ˜𝑠, 0) + b𝑃 ˜𝑠 0bℎ =

b𝜆 = 𝑟 ( ˜𝑠, 0) − 𝑟 ( ˜𝑠, 1) − b𝜉 bℎ. (29) Finally, as noted in the proof of Lemma 14, the equality of the objective values for the optimal solutions to the primal and dual LPs implies that 𝜁 ★ = 𝜇 ★𝑊𝑟 + 𝜆 ★𝛼 , so we define b𝜁 as 

b𝜁 = b𝜇𝑊𝑟 + b𝜆𝛼, (30) 29 

so that the perturbed dual objective matches the objective value of the candidate primal solution. Now having defined (b𝜁, b𝜆, bℎ), we find conditions for their dual feasibility. The only constraint which we need to check is (12b) . We check each row of (12b) , dividing them into two cases based on whether the constraint will hold with equality or inequality. First we handle the equality case. 

Lemma 15. Suppose that 𝑈 − 1𝜇 ★ 

> 𝜇 ★→𝜇 ★

< 1 and that (25) holds. Then we have 𝑟 𝜋 ★

This lemma shows that the dual constraints are active on the support of 𝑦 ★. However, this is not yet enough for dual feasibility. We still need to check that the dual constraints also hold for the inactive pairs (𝑠, 𝑎 ) with 𝑦 ★(𝑠, 𝑎 ) = 0. The next lemma shows that the dual variables only change by a small amount, so dual constraints still have positive slacks after perturbation.

The first part of the lemma controls how much the dual variables move. The last inequality shows that every inactive pair (𝑠, 𝑎 ) still has positive dual slack after perturbation. Combining this with Lemma 15, the perturbed dual constraints are tight on the support of 𝑦 ★ and strictly slack outside the support. Hence the candidate dual solution is feasible, and together with the primal solution from Section 8.3, we obtain these candidates have the same objective value and satisfy complementary slackness. Therefore they are optimal for the perturbed primal and dual LPs. Lemma 15 and Lemma 16 are proved in E.5. 30 

8.5. Completing the proof of Theorem 3 

First we confirm the optimality of the constructed candidate solutions b𝑦 and (b𝜁, b𝜆, bℎ) for their respective LPs. Lemma 13 guarantees primal feasibility of b𝑦 , and Lemmas 15 and 16 guarantee dual feasibility of (b𝜁, b𝜆, bℎ). Since by construction these primal and dual solutions have the same objective value of b𝜇𝑊𝑟 = b𝜁 − 𝛼 b𝜆 , they are both optimal. The next lemma shows the uniqueness of both the primal and dual solutions and is proved in E.6. 

Lemma 17. The primal candidate solution b𝑦 and dual candidate solutions (b𝜁, b𝜆, bℎ) are unique. 

The following lemma which is proved in E.7 checks the perturbation bounds from the statement of the theorem and confirm that all assumptions are satisfied for all the lemmas used in the proof. 

Lemma 18. Under the conditions of Theorem 3, the perturbation bounds in Part 4 hold. Moreover, the technical conditions (25) and (27) used in the construction are satisfied. 

Now it remains to prove the Markov-chain conclusion. which is guaranteed by the following lemma and its proof is contained in E.8. 

Lemma 19. Under the conditions of Theorem 3, the single-arm Markov chain induced by b¯𝜋 ★

under b𝑃 is aperiodic and irreducible. Its stationary distribution is b𝜇 ★(𝑠 ) = b𝑦 ★(𝑠, 0) + b𝑦 ★(𝑠, 1), and its mixing time is at most (3 + log 2 𝑆 )𝜏 .

We now match the numbered conclusions in the theorem. Part 1 follows from the support preservation in Lemma 13. Since the support is unchanged, the neutral state ˜𝑠 is also preserved. Part 2 is the lower bound b𝑦 ≥ 𝑦 ★/2 from Lemma 13. Part 3 follows from Lemma 12. After support preservation, the perturbed locally linear policy is the one induced by b¯𝜋 ★, so b𝑈 − 1b𝜇 is the perturbed local-stability matrix bΦ. Part 4 follows from Lemma 13 and Lemma 16, together with b𝑈 − 𝑈 ∞→∞ ≤ 3𝛿, b𝜉 − 𝜉 ∞→∞ ≤ 2𝛿. Part 5 is the strict-gap conclusion in Lemma 16, after using the support preservation from Part 1. Finally, Part 6 is Lemma 19. This proves all parts of Theorem 3.

𝐴 , and 1/𝜂 .

Appendix C: Preliminaries for the proof of Theorem 1 

Theorem 1 is based on the ID policy, proposed by Zhang et al. (2025). To facilitate the presentation of the proof of Theorem 1, in this section we provide some preliminaries on the ID policy and its analysis. The content is mostly taken from Zhang et al. (2025). We present the description of ID policy in Section C.1, the analysis of ID policy in Section C.2, and the empirical ID policy in Section C.3. 38 

C.1. Description of the ID policy 

In this subsection, we describe the ID policy and the corresponding ID reassignment subroutine.

C.2. Analysis of the ID policy 

The analysis of the ID policy in Zhang et al. (2025) relies on a specific Lyapunov function. Before we introduce the Lyapunov function, we firstly introduce the one-hot representation of the state and define a key quantity called the focus set. 

One-hot representation. For a system state 𝒔 = (𝑠 𝑖 )𝑖 ∈[ 𝑁 ] , let 𝒙 ∈ R𝑁 ×𝑆 be its one-hot representation, where 𝑥 𝑖 (𝑠 ) = I{𝑠 𝑖 = 𝑠 }. We use 𝒔 and 𝒙 interchangeably, and write 𝑿 𝑡 for the one-hot representation of the stochastic state 𝑺 𝑡 .

Focus set. The ID policy executes ideal actions only for a (random) prefix of arms of length 𝑁 ★𝑡 .To analyze this, the paper introduces a focus set that tracks a deterministic prefix length selected based on the current system state. Let 𝒙 denote a system state and let ℎ(𝒙 , 𝑚 ) be a Lyapunov-type quantity which is parameterized by 𝑚 ∈ [ 0, 1]𝑁 ≜ {0, 1/𝑁, . . . , 1} and is defined in (35). Define 

𝑚 (𝒙 ) = max 

n

𝑚 ∈ [ 0, 1]𝑁 : ℎ(𝒙 , 𝑚 ) ≤ min 

> 𝑘 ∈[ 𝐾 ]

¯𝐶 ★𝑘 ([ 𝑁𝑚 ]) 

o

. (33) 40 

and the focus set as the prefix [𝑁𝑚 (𝑥 )] . Intuitively, 𝑚 (𝑥 ) is chosen as the largest fraction such that 

ℎ(𝒙 , 𝑚 ) is covered by the minimum remaining budget across constraints. The analysis then establishes three key properties: (i) majority conformity —almost all arms in the focus set, except for 𝑂 (√𝑁 ) arms, can follow the optimal single-armed policies ¯𝜋 ★𝑖 ; (ii) 

almost non-shrinking —the focus set shrinks by at most 𝑂 (√𝑁 ) on average over time; (iii) sufficient coverage —the complement size 1 − 𝑚 ( 𝑿 𝑡 ) is controlled by ℎ( 𝑿 𝑡 , 𝑚 ( 𝑿 𝑡 )) .

Projection-based Lyapunov function. We firstly introduce some necessary single-armed quantities. For each cost type 𝑘 ∈ [ 𝐾 ], define 𝑐 ★𝑘,𝑖 (𝑠 ) ≜ Í𝑎 ∈A ¯𝜋 ★𝑖 (𝑎 | 𝑠 ) 𝑐 𝑘,𝑖 (𝑠, 𝑎 ) and the vector 𝑐 ★𝑘 ≜ (𝑐 ★𝑘,𝑖 )𝑖 ∈[ 𝑁 ] .Similarly, define 𝑟 ★𝑖 (𝑠 ) ≜ Í𝑎 ∈A ¯𝜋 ★𝑖 (𝑎 | 𝑠 ) 𝑟 𝑖 (𝑠, 𝑎 ) and 𝑟 ★ ≜ (𝑟 ★𝑖 )𝑖 ∈[ 𝑁 ] . C.3. Empirical ID policy 

In this subsection, we introduce the ID policy for the empirical system. The empirical LP is obtained from the relaxation (2) by replacing 𝑃 𝑖 with b𝑃 𝑖 in the flow-balance constraints. Let b𝜌 rel be its optimal value, and let  b𝑦 ★𝑖 (𝑠, 𝑎 ) 

> 𝑖 ∈[ 𝑁 ], 𝑠 ∈S , 𝑎 ∈A

be an optimal solution. The empirical optimal single-arm policies {b¯𝜋 ★𝑖 }𝑖 ∈[ 𝑁 ] are defined from b𝑦 ★𝑖 in the same way that { ¯𝜋 ★𝑖 }𝑖 ∈[ 𝑁 ] are defined from 𝑦 ★𝑖 . Plugging 

b𝑦 ★𝑖 and b¯𝜋 ★𝑖 into the ID-policy construction gives the empirical ID policy b𝜋 ID . For any policy 𝜋 , we write b𝜌 𝜋 ∈ R𝑆 𝑁 

for its gain under the empirical 𝑁 -armed system.

D.2. Proof of Lemma 4 

We prove Lemma 4. The goal of Lemma 4 is to bound b𝜌 rel − 𝜌 b𝜋 ID (𝒔 0), which is the second term in the decomposition (15) , so we need to work with the empirical system. The proof mirrors that of Lemma 3, but with the empirical LP solution and the empirical kernel. We first verify that the empirical single-arm chains needed to define the empirical Lyapunov function are well behaved. 

D.2.1. Markov chain under empirical model 

Lemma 24. Under the conditions of Lemma 4, for every 𝑖 ∈ [ 𝑁 ], the Markov chain induced by 

b¯𝜋 ★𝑖 under b𝑃 𝑖 is unichain and aperiodic, with mixing time at most 𝜏 , where 𝜏 is defined in (34) .

𝑠, 𝑎 ). Although b¯𝜋 ★𝑖 is computed from the empirical model, it is still a stationary policy for the true single-arm MDP. Hence Assumption 1 implies that 𝑄 𝑖 is unichain, aperiodic, and has mixing time at most 𝜏 unif . Moreover, by convexity of the ℓ1 norm, 

b𝑄 𝑖 − 𝑄 𝑖 ∞→∞ ≤ max

Lemma 20, applied with Z = S, 𝑄 = 𝑄 𝑖 , and e𝑄 = b𝑄 𝑖 , gives the desired conclusion. Let b𝜇 ★𝑖 denote the stationary distribution of the Markov chain induced by b¯𝜋 ★𝑖 under b𝑃 𝑖 , whose existence and uniqueness are guaranteed by Lemma 24. 43

E.2. Algebraic properties of 𝑈, b𝑈, 𝜇 ★, b𝜇, 𝐻 𝑈 and 𝐻 b𝑈

Lemma 27. We have 𝑈 1 = 1 and b𝑈 1 = 1. Moreover, if 𝐼 − ( 𝑈 − 1𝜇 ★) is invertible, then 𝐻 𝑈 1 = 0.

is row-stochastic and

− 𝛼 1)( 𝜉 1) = 1.

(𝐼 − ( 𝑈 − 1𝜇 ★)) 𝑥 = (𝐼 − ( 𝑈 − 1𝜇 ★))( 𝐼 − ( 𝑈 − 1𝜇 ★)) −11 − ( 𝐼 − ( 𝑈 − 1𝜇 ★)) 1 = 1 − 1 + 𝑈 1 − 1𝜇 ★1 = 0

E.4. Proof of Lemma 14 

Proof. Each row of 𝑊 is only supported on indices 𝑖 such that 𝑦 ★𝑖 > 0, and hence where (28) holds. Therefore we have 𝑊 (𝑟 + 𝜆 ★ ˜𝑒 + 𝑃ℎ ★) = 𝑊 (𝜁 ★1 + 𝐽ℎ ★). 𝜁 ★1 + ℎ★. Eliminating 𝜁 ★ using that 𝜁 ★ = 𝜇 ★𝑊𝑟 + 𝜆 ★𝛼 (since the primal value 𝑦 ★𝑟 = 𝜇 ★𝑊𝑟 must be equal to the dual value 𝜉 ★ − 𝛼𝜆 ★), we obtain 𝑊𝑟 + 𝜆 ★𝛼 1 + 𝑈ℎ ★ = (𝜇 ★𝑊𝑟 + 𝜆 ★𝛼 )1 + ℎ★ which simplifies further to (𝐼 − 𝑈 )ℎ★ = 𝑊𝑟 − 𝜇 ★𝑊𝑟 1. The solutions to this Poisson-like equation can be characterized using the assumed invertibility of (𝐼 − ( 𝑈 − 1𝜇 ★)) . Rewriting the equation as 

 𝐼 − ( 𝑈 − 1𝜇 ★) ℎ★ = (𝐼 − 1𝜇 ★)𝑊𝑟 + 1𝜇 ★ℎ, we obtain 

ℎ★ =  𝐼 − ( 𝑈 − 1𝜇 ★)−1  (𝐼 − 1𝜇 ★)𝑊𝑟 + 1𝜇 ★ℎ★

=  𝐼 − ( 𝑈 − 1𝜇 ★)−1 𝑊𝑟 − 1𝜇 ★𝑊𝑟 + 1𝜇 ★ℎ★ = 𝐻 𝑈 𝑊𝑟 + 1𝜇 ★ℎ★

using that  𝐼 − ( 𝑈 − 1𝜇 ★)−1 1 = 1, which follows from the fact that 𝐻 𝑈 1 = 0 (Lemma 27).

E.6. Proof of Lemma 17 

Proof of Lemma 17. Now we show uniqueness of the primal and dual optimal solutions. First, letting ˜𝑦 be another optimal solution to the perturbed primal LP, then ˜𝑦 and (b𝜁, b𝜆, bℎ) must satisfy the complementary slackness property (13) (as optimal solutions). Since we have checked in Lemma 16 that the dual constraints are strict for all 𝑠, 𝑎 such that 𝑦 ★(𝑠, 𝑎 ) = 0, this implies that we must have that ˜𝑦 (𝑠, 𝑎 ) = 0 for all 𝑠, 𝑎 such that 𝑦 ★(𝑠, 𝑎 ) = 0. This implies that, letting ˜𝜇 (𝑠 ) = ˜𝑦 (𝑠, 0) + ˜𝑦 (𝑠, 1),54 

we have that ˜𝑦 = ˜𝜇𝑊 , since ˜𝑦 is supported on a (possibly non-proper) subset of the support of 𝑦 ★,and also ˜𝑦 ˜𝑒 = 𝛼 by its feasibility for the primal perturbed LP. Then since primal feasibility also guarantees ˜𝑦𝑃 = ˜𝑦𝐽 , we have ˜𝜇 b𝑈 = ˜𝜇𝑊 b𝑃 = ˜𝑦 b𝑃 = ˜𝑦𝐽 = ˜𝜇𝑊 𝐽 = ˜𝜇𝐼 = ˜𝜇. From the fact that ˜𝜇 b𝑈 = ˜𝜇 ,we can derive that 

˜𝜇 ( b𝑈 − 1b𝜇 ) = ˜𝜇 b𝑈 − ˜𝜇 1b𝜇 = ˜𝜇 − b𝜇 =⇒ b𝜇 = ˜𝜇 



𝐼 − ( b𝑈 − 1b𝜇 )



=⇒ ˜𝜇 = b𝜇 



𝐼 − ( b𝑈 − 1b𝜇 )

−1

= b𝜇𝐻 b𝑈 + b𝜇 1b𝜇 = b𝜇 

using that ˜𝜇 1 = ˜𝜇𝑊 1 = ˜𝑦 1 = 1 by primal feasibility of ˜𝑦 in the first line, and then invertibility of 

𝐼 − ( b𝑈 − 1b𝜇 ) (by Lemma 12) and then the facts that b𝜇𝐻 b𝑈 = 0 and b𝜇 1 = 1 (both from Lemma 28) in the final line. Finally, b𝜇 = ˜𝜇 implies that ˜𝑦 = ˜𝜇𝑊 = b𝜇𝑊 = b𝑦 , so we have shown uniqueness of b𝑦 .Now we show the uniqueness properties of the dual solution. These steps are analogous to those of Lemma 14. Let ( ˜𝜁, ˜𝜆, ˜ℎ) be some dual optimal solution. By complementary slackness between 

( ˜𝜁, ˜𝜆, ˜ℎ) and b𝑦 , for all 𝑠, 𝑎 such that b𝑦 (𝑠, 𝑎 ) > 0 we have 𝑟 (𝑠, 𝑎 ) + ˜𝜆 I(𝑎 = 1) + b𝑃 𝑠𝑎 ˜ℎ = ˜𝜁 + ˜ℎ(𝑠 ). By the construction of b𝑦 and Lemma 13, these are exactly the 𝑠, 𝑎 such that 𝑦 ★(𝑠, 𝑎 ) > 0. Each row of

F.3. Weighted ℓ2 norms for quantifying distributional convergence 

A recurring theme in our analysis is to control how fast the empirical state distribution of a collection of arms approaches a target stationary distribution. To this end, we follow the weighted-ℓ2 approach and introduce two matrices, 𝑊 and 𝑈 , which is used in Hong et al. (2024a) and induce two weighted norms on R𝑆 . 𝑃 , and the matrix Φ is defined in (6).

F.6. Maximal Feasible Set 

In our algorithm, at each time step we select a subset of arms on which a local control routine is executed. Rather than requiring an exact maximal feasible set, we work with an approximate notion of maximality, parameterized by a small tolerance 𝜖 rd  

> 𝑁

≥ 0. This tolerance allows mild slack in the maximality requirement while still ensuring that the chosen set is nearly as large as any sufficiently feasible superset. 

Definition 3 ( 𝜖 rd  

> 𝑁

-maximal feasible set). Fix a system state 𝒙 and a tolerance parameter 𝜖 rd  

> 𝑁

≥ 0.A set 𝐷 ⊆ [ 𝑁 ] is called 𝜖 rd  

> 𝑁

-maximal feasible at state 𝒙 if the following hold: 1. Feasibility: 

𝛿 (𝒙 , 𝐷 ) ≥ 0, or 𝐷 = ∅; 2. Approximate maximality: for any 𝐷 ′ such that 𝐷 ⊆ 𝐷 ′ ⊆ [ 𝑁 ] and 

𝛿 (𝒙 , 𝐷 ′) ≥ 𝜖 rd  

> 𝑁

, we have 𝑚 (𝐷 ′) ≤ 𝑚 (𝐷 ) + 𝜖 rd  

> 𝑁

.

When 𝜖 rd  

> 𝑁

= 0, Definition 3 reduces to the usual notion of a maximal element (under set inclusion) in the family {𝐷 ′ ⊆ [ 𝑁 ] : 𝛿 (𝒙 , 𝐷 ′) ≥ 0} ∪ {∅} . In our theoretical development we typically take 

𝜖 rd  

> 𝑁

= Θ (1/𝑁 ), matching the granularity of the mass 𝑚 (𝐷 ), since 𝑚 (𝐷 ) changes in increments of 

1/𝑁 when adding or removing a single arm. 60
